Question No 19 by Katerina Daskalaki (H−0454/96)

Subject: Infringement of international treaties by Turkey

Is the Council aware of the Turkish Government's recent decision to put a further four hundred settlers on the island of Imbros (in addition to the 2000 convicts already housed there in expropriated Greek properties)? Is the Council also aware that Greek property is still being expropriated and that the few Greeks remaining on the island continue to be intimidated, while their families' inheritance rights are not recognized?

Given that Turkey is an associate member of the Union with special status, does the Council intend to take any action on this matter?

Question No 20 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H−0471/96)

Subject: Violation of human rights and international treaties on the island of Imbros

It was reported recently that the inhabitants of an entire Turkish village are to be transferred as settlers to the island of Imbros. This is the latest in a long series of similar measures aimed at de‐hellenizing the island whose Greek population has dropped from 6 000 in 1950 to 300 today. This is undoubtedly a flagrant violation of human rights and international treaties and in particular of Article 14 of the Treaty of Lausanne which provides that the islands of Imbros and Tenedos shall be placed under a special administration consisting of local people and providing every safeguard for the indigenous non‐Muslim population as regards local self‐government and the protection of persons and property. Given that none of these provisions are respected in practice, can the Council say how it views this situation and what measures it intends to take to ensure that human rights and international law are respected?

Joint answer

The Council is not aware of a recent decision by the Turkish Government as referred to by the honourable Member.

The Turkish human rights situation remains an issue of concern to the Council, which spares no efforts to emphasize it to the Turkish authorities in all the possible contacts.

As far as property matters are concerned, the Council has no power to intervene in a private law dispute in a third country.

Question No 21 by Luciano Pettinari (H−0456/96)

Subject: US extraterritorial policy and relations with Cuba

On 24 May 1996 the European Parliament unanimously adopted a resolution calling on the United States to stop passing trade legislation against Cuba, Iran and Libya, the extraterritorial effects of which are detrimental to important European economic and financial interests. One instance of this is the inclusion of the Italian STET company in the United States' black list because of its economic and trade relations with Cuba.

What steps has the Council taken to protest to the United States against this situation and urge the US Congress to stop adopting extraterritorial trade legislation? How does the Council view the specific case of the STET company?
How does the Council view the current deadlock in negotiations for an economic and trade cooperation agreement between the EU and Cuba?

Answer

1. On 22 April 1996, the Council adopted a declaration on the Helms‐Burton legislation and other proposed US legislation with extraterritorial effects. On that occasion, the Council expressed its deep regret at that legislation which it considers to be incompatible with international law and detrimental to the interests of the European Union in the trade and investment sector.

The Council has also asked experts in the field to consider all of the options, within the WTO or other bodies, for the Union to take initiatives to protect its rights and interests – initiatives which could involve countermeasures.

2. In particular, in the context of the WTO, the Union decided to ask for consultations with the United States, in accordance with Articles XXIII of the GATT and XXIII of the GATS. Those consultations began on 4 June 1996 in Geneva.

The various Council bodies are also looking at other ways and means of achieving the objectives set down in the above‐mentioned declaration.

In the context of the OECD and, in particular, the negotiations under way for the conclusion of a multilateral investment agreement, the Union has undertaken to seek the agreement of all of the contracting parties on rules formally prohibiting the extraterritorial application of the legislation of any one of them.

3. As regards the specific case of STET, I can confirm that this holding company in the telecommunications sector has already received a warning from the US authorities concerning its economic activities in Cuba.

The Council is monitoring closely this issue, which threatens the interests of a Member State – and therefore those of the Union – and will not fail to respond appropriately as the situation develops.

4. As regards the negotiations for the conclusion of an economic and trade cooperation agreement between the European Union and Cuba, the Madrid European Council (14‐15 December 1995) considered it appropriate to continue the dialogue and cooperation with that country and actively support the process of reform under way, to encourage respect for human rights and fundamental freedoms and to extend the scope of private initiative and the development of a civilized society. To that end, it invited the Commission to submit a draft mandate for an economic and trade cooperation agreement during the first half of 1996.

Since then, the Commission has been in close contact with the Cuban authorities at both a political and an economic level. Following those contacts, on 8 May 1996, Commissioner Marín sent to the President‐in‐Office a letter stating that, in the light of the responses obtained from the Cuban negotiators in the course of the above‐mentioned contacts, the Commission was not in a position to transit to the Council, within the time‐limit laid down, draft negotiating guidelines for an agreement between the Union and Cuba. In that same letter, Commissioner Marín proposed continuing and extending the dialogue currently under way with the Cuban authorities.

Question No 22 by Jan Mulder (H−0458/96)

Subject: European contribution arrangements for outbreaks of infectious plant diseases such as brown rot and Thrips palmi

Last year it again became apparent how extensive are the financial losses incurred by the agricultural sector from the outbreak of highly infectious plant diseases like brown rot and Thrips Palmi. The Italian presidency of the Council has informed me, in answer to my written question, that it has planned two meetings at which the proposal will be considered.

Can the Council state how soon these two meetings are to be held?

Can the Council inform me of the substance of the compromise proposal?

Answer

During the first half of this year, several meetings of the Agricultural Affairs Group – on 1 March, 8 May and 7 June – have been devoted to examining the proposal to which the honourable Member refers. A compromise text has been drawn up by the presidency and will be submitted shortly to the Committee of Permanent Representatives, which will be preparing the Council discussions.

Without going into the details of this compromise text, which are very numerous and technical, it seems essential to point out that the main stumbling‐block, namely the procedure laid down for the reimbursement of expenses and for compensation in respect of loss or damage, which has hitherto prevented an agreement, has now been overcome. In this context, the compromise text actually refers now to national legislation, generally Community law and international law and ought therefore to be acceptable to the delegations and the Commission.

Question No 26 by Hans Lindqvist (H−0472/96)

Subject: Border protection between EU Member States

Several large herds of animals in Sweden have had to be slaughtered to prevent the spread of infectious diseases brought here from the rest of Europe. The cost of eradicating animal diseases has increased five‐fold in two years to almost ECU 5.5 million. This year spending is expected to soar again; during the first four months of the year alone the cost was nearly ECU 5 million. During the same period meat imports into Sweden have increased. Sweden has been allowed to retain its previous quarantine rules for cattle for a transition period but the quarantine requirement will probably disappear this year. This will mean fewer veterinary checks at borders, allowing even more infected animals into the country. This is only the tip of the iceberg. More stringent checks on fodder and an improvement in animals' ‚living conditions’ are important. It is not normal to feed cows animal remains as feed additives any more than it is to stack ‚battery hens’ on top of each other. We must take measures to combat the developments we have had to witness in recent times, the worst example being ‚mad cow disease’.

Is the Council prepared to take the initiative in establishing stricter border controls, on a permanent basis, between the Member States for both slaughtered and live animals? Will the Council take steps to promote a better quality of animal husbandry in the EU?

Answer

1. The Single Act abolished border controls between Member States.

In the veterinary sector the conditions concerning abolition of border controls were laid down by Directives 89/662/EEC and 90/425/EEC, which oblige the veterinary authorities in the Member State of dispatch to check compliance with the health requirements of the Member State of destination, including compliance with the additional guarantees recognised by Community legislation. The veterinary authorities of the country of destination may carry out non‐discriminatory spot checks.

Those principles were supplemented by provisions concerning mutual assistance between veterinary authorities.

On the basis of those principles, under the ‚acquis communautaire’, the Member States have re‐imposed certain checks on a temporary basis and the provision of certain additional guarantees.

2. With particular regard to measures concerning stockfarming conditions in the Union, it should be pointed out that:

‐ Member States have banned the use of ruminant meat and bonemeal as feed for ruminants;

‐ Directive 88/166/EEC governs the conditions for keeping laying hens in battery cages;

‐ the conditions for rearing calves are governed by Directive 91/629/EEC, for which implementing texts were proposed by the Commission on 9 February 1996.

***

Question No 27 by Liam Hyland (H−0479/96)

Subject: Biomass fuels

What action has the Council of Ministers taken to increase the use of alternative renewable energy sources, in particular biomass fuels, and will it give priority to the development of a comprehensive European policy on renewable energy?

Answer

1. The Council can assure the honourable Member that it is fully aware of the importance of developing the use of alternative renewable energy sources.

2. In this connection it would refer him to its Decision concerning the promotion of renewable energy sources in the Community (ALTENER programme), which it adopted on 13 September 1993. In that Decision the Council took due account of the concerns regarding biomass fuels which are mentioned in the question.

In fact, Article 1 of that Decision specifies that ‚Member States shall endeavour to contribute in their energy policies to the limitation of carbon dioxide emissions by taking account of the Community's indicative objectives relating to the renewable energy sources which are set out in Annex I’. It should be underlined in this context that point C of the said Annex lays down the Community objective of ‚securing for biofuels a market share of 5 % of total fuel consumption by motor vehicles. The production in 2005 of 11 million ton oil equivalents of biofuels is considered necessary in order to achieve this objective’.

3. The Council will examine the situation in that field as soon as the Commission has communicated to the Council its new proposal for continuing the current ALTENER programme, which is due to expire at the end of 1997.

4. Irrespective of that specific programme, the Council wishes to point out that it has been continuing its general deliberations on renewable energy sources.

On the basis of the Green Paper and of the White Paper on an energy policy for the European Union, which were submitted by the Commission, the Council adopted two resolutions, in 1995 and 1996 respectively, which incorporate the renewability dimension into its general energy policy deliberations.

In its resolution of 23 November 1995, the Council considered that one of the purposes of greater convergence of energy policies within the European Union should be the promotion of new and renewable energy sources and indigenous resources with a view to protecting the environment and reducing energy dependence at an acceptable economic cost.

The resolution adopted by the Council of Energy Ministers at their latest meeting on 7 May 1996 reaffirmed the need to promote new and renewable energy sources and energy efficiency as factors that could make a valuable contribution to creating a safer and healthier environment and, together with the development of indigenous resources, to reducing the dependence of the Community on external energy sources and promoting economic growth and employment. The resolution also stressed the need to make use of the full range of Community instruments and to cooperate with national initiatives in developing indigenous and renewable sources of energy. Particular emphasis was placed on the formulation of standardization programmes designed to promote new and renewable energy sources and on the more coherent formulation of programmes devoted to energy efficiency and to new and renewable energy sources with a view to making more effective use of budgetary resources.

5. The Council has every intention of continuing its work in this field, particularly once it has received the Communication on a strategy to promote the market penetration of energy from renewable sources which is envisaged in the Commission's programme of work for 1996.

Question No 28 by Robert Evans (H−0482/96)

Subject: China and human rights abuses

What pressures are the EU and its individual members applying on China in order to encourage the cessation of the continuing human rights abuses there?

Answer

The Council monitors with great attention the human rights situation in China as in all other countries, and does not hesitate to deplore and condemn violations of those rights, whenever they occur, in its contacts with the Chinese authorities, and to take up the issue in the relevant international fora.

In its bilateral exchanges with the Chinese authorities the EU has always given the utmost importance to the human rights aspect and has represented to the Chinese authorities, several times since January 1995, its concerns about, inter alia, the excessive use of capital punishment, the treatment of political dissidents, the questions of the orphanages and the situation in Tibet. The decision to hold meetings at expert level on these issues has been helpful in developing that dialogue. It is the EU's firm intention to pursue this dialogue in a positive, non‐confrontational way.

The honourable Member will be aware of the fact that the EU has, in the same spirit, submitted a draft resolution on the human rights situation in China at the 52nd session of the Commission on Human Rights on 18 March−26 April 1996. The vote on the EU text, co‐sponsored by several other countries, was prevented by a ‚no‐action’ motion, called for by China, and supported by the majority of the members of the Commission on Human Rights.

The EU actively promoted its resolution, refuting the Chinese accusations of interference in Chinese internal affairs and underlining that this and other EU initiatives at the CHR were an expression of concern for violations of human rights wherever they may occur.

In doing so, the Council had been encouraged by the resolution on the draft resolution on China, adopted by the European Parliament at its plenary session of 15‐19 April 1996.

Nevertheless, the Council remains all the more committed to the objectives set out in the draft resolution and will actively pursue the bilateral dialogue with the Chinese authorities. To that end, the Council is currently reflecting on the most appropriate steps to achieve those objectives.

***

Question No 29 by Maj Theorin (H−0484/96)

Subject: The bugging scandal in Romania

In May 1996, the Romanian press uncovered a major bugging scandal. A security service agent who had defected revealed that the conversations of members of the opposition were systematically intercepted. It takes time to build up democratic structures in a country. In the Balkans, the situation is further complicated owing to internal ethnic conflicts in various countries. Both Albania and Croatia have recently been criticized for not fulfilling the criteria for what may be described as democratic states.

With this scandal still so fresh in our memories, it must be borne in mind that local elections are due to be held in Romania in June, with the presidential and general elections taking place later in the autumn. Romania is a potential candidate for EU membership. What is the Council doing to monitor developments in the political field and as regards democracy in the run‐up to the elections. In its negotiations with Romania, how does it apply the democratic requirements which are a feature of the accession strategy in order to ensure that Romania fulfils the relevant criteria for a stable and properly functioning democracy?

Answer

The membership criteria established at the 1993 European Council meeting in Copenhagen apply to all twelve candidate countries, including Romania.

The Romanian Government took the initiative of inviting the Union to monitor the local elections that took place on 2 June, and a similar invitation is expected for the presidential and parliamentary elections to be held on 3 November.

The Council has received no reports of irregularities in connection with the local elections. The main problem with these elections seems to have been the regrettably low voter turnout.

Question No 30 by Caroline Jackson (H−0489/96)

Subject: EU finance for tobacco production

Will the Council now give as high a priority to bringing to an end EU finance for tobacco production – a known carcinogen presently costing EU taxpayers more than 950m ECU per annum – as it is to the question of BSE, whose impact on human health is not proven?

Answer

The Council is aware of the problems created for public health by smoking as demonstrated by the many acts which it has adopted on the subject:

1. Resolution 89/C 189/01 on the prohibition of smoking in places open to the public,
2. Directive 89/622/EEC on the approximation of the laws, regulations and administrative provisions of the Member States concerning the labelling of tobacco products,
3. Directive 90/239/EEC the approximation of the laws, regulations and administrative provisions of the Member States concerning the maximum tar yield of cigarettes,
4. Council conclusions of 13 November 1992 on reducing the use of tobacco,
5. Conclusions 93 C 174/02 on the response to the resolution on the prohibition of smoking in places open to the public,
6. Several acts relating to the fight against cancer.

With regard to tobacco production in the European Union, the Council would point out that this product features in Annex II to the EEC Treaty and is therefore covered by the common agricultural policy provisions set forth in Article 39 et seq. of that Treaty.

The market in tobacco is therefore subject to common organization, and the aid given to tobacco producers under that regime is entirely in line with the aims enshrined in Article 39 of the Treaty, as is all other aid granted under the common agricultural policy.

The production of tobacco is traditionally confined to particular regions of the Community, where there is no obvious substitute cash crop.

Moreover, tobacco‐growing is highly labour‐intensive and is therefore a means of combating unemployment, especially in the poor or underprivileged regions where tobacco production is concentrated.

It should also be noted that there are no restrictions on imports of tobacco and tobacco products from third countries as Community tobacco production accounts for only 30 % of the requirements of Community industry; if Community producers were unable to compete with external producers, the latter would corner the entire tobacco market in the European Union.

The problems caused by bovine spongiform encephalopathy and the risks to human health from that disease are totally different from those created by tobacco. The fact is that the outbreak of this disease has had dire consequences for the Community beef and veal market, which provides meat to satisfy consumer demand and a decent income for producers in every Member State. It was therefore necessary to take appropriate emergency measures to combat this cattle disease and thus to safeguard beef and veal production, producers' income and public health.

Question No 38 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0291/96)

Subject: Memorandum on classical studies in Europe

The memorandum on classical studies was submitted to the meeting of the Member States' Education Ministers on 31 March 1995. At the conference held in Athens on 30 June 1995 on this subject, representatives of the Member States' Education Ministers drew up a timetable for action.

The Education Ministers meeting in Luxembourg in October 1995 gave their backing to a European Centre for Classical Studies and the Italian Education Minister, Mr Lombardi, undertook to pursue the matter.

What is the next stage regarding the options for the European Centre for Classical Studies? Does the Council intend to make a token entry under the relevant article in the 1997 budget?

Answer

1. The memorandum to which the honourable Member refers in his question is the fruit of a Greek initiative aimed at creating a European Centre for Classical Studies, supported by a number of Member States on an intergovernmental basis.

2. As it happens – and the point is worth making – this is not a Community initiative resulting from the submission of a Commission proposal. At the present time no Commission proposal on this matter has been placed before the Council.

3. Moreover, it is not accurate to state that the Council approved the creation of the said centre at its meeting on 23 October 1995. On that occasion the Council merely took note of a presentation by the presidency on the subject.

5. Lastly, this item appeared on the agenda for the Education Council on 6 May. 6. The Council will, of course, give the closest attention to any report which the Permanent Representatives Committee may submit to it in this field.

Question No 39 by Anne McIntosh (H−0340/96)

Subject: Monetary Union

Is the Council aware of the likely consequences on employment of an eventual economic and monetary union?

Is any work being undertaken into the impact on employment, social and training problems caused by EMU?

Answer

Problems associated with unemployment are amongst the major concerns of the Council and the presidency.

It is useful in this connection to underline that the Heads of State and Government, meeting in Turin, reaffirmed the priority that they attach to employment and they have welcomed the presidency's intention to give greater importance to the discussion on that topic at the forthcoming European Council in Florence. On 14 and 15 June the presidency organized in Rome a Tripartite Conference on growth and employment with the participation of Governments, social partners and the Commission to prepare the work of the forthcoming Florence European Council.

These examples show clearly that the need to eradicate unemployment and to revitalize the labour market is at the heart of the action currently being implemented by the Community.

The presidency for its part will spare no effort to make a positive contribution to solving the unemployment problem since it considers that, at the dawn of the third millennium, marginalization of ever more numerous sections of the population is one of the most intractable social problems that our countries have to face.

In the eyes of the Council, creating economic and monetary union is an essential factor in improving the prospects of sustained production growth, which – as we all know – is the precondition for maintaining jobs and countering unemployment.

Economic and monetary union is a crucial tool for promoting financial stability and the correct operation of the internal market. It is also likely to create an adequate framework for healthy monetary and fiscal policies.

As the honourable Member can see, these are the necessary conditions for the economic revitalization which, if it was slow restarting, would only exacerbate the already unbearable tensions existing on the labour market.

The need to carry out a more finely tuned assessment of the consequences of setting up economic and monetary union has not escaped the Council's notice.

The Community has indeed equipped itself to examine certain more specific aspects of economic and monetary union, whose impact on the labour market is indubitably the most important.

This evaluation will be carried out mainly by using instruments such as the broad economic policy and multilateral monitoring guidelines, both before and after passing into the third stage of economic and monetary union.

II. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 52 by Hugh Kerr (H−0499/96)

Subject: Public services

The Commission is currently working on a communication on public services which we hope to see published by the July European Council. Can the Commission confirm that this communication will address the issue of Treaty changes to articles on European citizenship and Article 129a on consumer protection?

Question No 53 by Mark Watts (H−0500/96)

Subject: Public services

The Commission is currently working on a communication on public services which we hope to see published by the July European Council. Can the Commission confirm that this communication will address the issue of Treaty changes to the competition articles (Articles 90(2) and 90(3)) in a progressive and positive manner?

Joint answer

The Communication on public services which the Commission may soon be in a position to publish is intended to present and clarify the Commission's current and future policy as regards public services or services of general interest.

However, it is not possible at this stage to comment on the substance of the Communication in terms of any proposed amendments to the Treaty which it might contain.

***

Question No 54 by David Bowe (H−0501/96)

Subject: Public services

In the opinion of the Commission what areas of economic and social activity should be covered by universal public service provision, and what, in its opinion, defines universal public service?

Answer

The Commission has defined the concept of universal public service, notably in the telecommunications sphere, as the provision to the public, on the basis of guaranteed supply, minimum quality standards and reasonable pricing, of certain services which, given the characteristics of the area of economic or social activity in question, may be regarded as essential.

It believes that, as thus defined, universal public service provision is a concept that may be applied to other services of general benefit which constitute business activities. However, the Commission is aware that each area of economic and social activity presents different characteristics and that this concept cannot therefore be interpreted too rigidly.

It would seem that universal public service provision implies certain obligations:

‐ equality of access and non‐discriminatory treatment of consumers, which can be particularly cogent grounds for providing a universal public service,

‐ security and reliability of supply,

‐ continuity of service, and

‐ reasonable prices.

The Commission is currently preparing a communication with a view to outlining and explaining its current and future policy on the provision of universal public service or of services of general benefit.

***

Question No 55 by Richard Howitt (H−0504/96)

Subject: Merger between BP and Mobil

Could the Commission clarify its position on the proposed joint venture between BP and Mobil?

Does the Commission agree that the 2 000 to 3 000 job losses expected across Europe as a result of the merger, including 160 jobs at the Mobil Oil Refinery at Corringham, must be taken into consideration along with compliance to competition criteria when the decision is taken whether to allow ventures of this kind to take place?

Does the Commission also recognize that delays in resolving this venture have had a knock‐on effect on employment, by creating uncertainty amongst local contractors who carry out business for the two companies involved? Will it therefore undertake to resolve this issue as quickly as possible?

Answer

As of 7 June 1996 BP and Mobil Oil have not yet notified the creation of their joint venture to the Commission. They have informed the Commission that no contracts have been signed and that there are still negotiations taking place. Until the Commission receives a notification, it does not have a position on this joint venture.

Decisions on redundancies are made by the parties to the joint venture and cannot be reviewed by the Commission neither under Article 85 of the Treaty nor under the Merger Regulation. Although the Commission can take employment and other factors into account in its appraisal of a joint venture, Article 85 of the Treaty and the Merger Regulation require the Commission to reach its conclusion according to competition criteria. In any case, the criteria of Directives 129/75/EEC and 56/92/EEC must be respected if, for any reason, collective dismissals are being planned.

The Commission has not caused any delays in resolving this venture as the Commission cannot act before the parties have ended their contract negotiations. Once it has received the notification, the Commission will issue a decision as quickly as possible whilst respecting the right of third parties to make representations.

***

Question No 62 by Sören Wibe (H−0432/96)

Subject: Ban on the breeding of Belgian blues in Sweden

Sweden's Agricultural Agency and Minister for Agriculture take the view that the rearing of Belgian blues is incompatible with the Swedish law on the prevention of cruelty to animals. Swedish farmers have been sued for having begun breeding Belgian blues.

Can Sweden be granted a derogation and introduce a ban on the breeding of Belgian blues?

Answer

The Commission's position on this question has not changed.

Under the Community provisions on stock‐breeding, Belgian blues are recognized as a breed compatible with the legal provisions on the free movement of goods in the Community.

In the Community's view, Member States are not entitled to ban the use of this breed of animal because of presumed infringements of animal protection provisions.

The Commission has no intention of taking action on the basis of animal protection laws against breeding programmes that are established in breed‐specific stud‐books.

If the Swedish authorities have banned the use of this species of bull for breeding purposes, that would at first sight represent an infringement of existing Community legislation.

The Commission will now lodge a complaint on the matter with the Swedish Government and if appropriate initiate the regulative procedure provided for in the Treaty in order to ensure compliance with the Community provisions.

***

Question No 63 by Miguel Arias Cañete (H−0436/96)

Subject: Disease on avocado pear plantations originating from Mexico

According to recent reports from California, a new disease originating in Mexico (Oligonychus perseae) has reached and is damaging plantations of avocado pears in that state.

The large volumes of avocado pears imported from Mexico – apart from the damage they cause to Community producers who cannot compete with their low prices – are produced without any kind of health inspection.

Is the Commission aware of the danger of this lack of health controls and of the risk both to European Union consumers and to the Community avocado pear sector, which may discover at any moment that this disease has reached its plantations? What measures does the Commission plan to take?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the problem of the avocado pear pest infecting plantations in Mexico.

This mite damages the avocado tree by defoliating it, but does not attack the fruit. That is why there is no risk to the consumer.

The use of specific mite‐control preparations and beneficial insects is recommended in order to reduce the damage caused by the mites.

That is why the Commission has not taken any precautions against this pest to date. The Community plantinspection rules do, however, provide for measures to protect the Community against the introduction and spread of fruit flies, which also infest avocados.

These provisions are currently being reviewed in order to adjust them to the most recent scientific findings.

Question No 64 by José Apolinário (H−0437/96)

Subject: Reform of the olive oil sector

Can the Commission say whether it is true that it proposes to make a grant of ECU 2.5 per olive‐tree in Portugal and ECU 4.5 per olive‐tree in Spain and, if so, can it explain this incomprehensible proposal and the thinking which guides the Commission in its COM for olive oil?

Answer

The Commission is currently drafting a reform of the COM for olive oil.

This draft reform will be based on experience of the way the existing COM has functioned in recent years. Its aim will be to guarantee producers a support system and at the same time simplify the rules and make them transparent and guarantee the environmental function of olive‐growing and the quality of the product.

When drawing up this draft reform the Commission will of course take account of Member States' views as expressed at the informal meeting of the Special Committee on Agriculture on 5 and 6 May in Otranto, Italy.

Question No 65 by Birgitta Ahlqvist (H−0438/96)

Subject: Origin labelling of foodstuffs

Swedish consumers consider it important to be able to see where foodstuffs have been produced. In 1988, following pressure from consumers, Sweden tightened up its law on the prevention of cruelty to animals. Hen‐coops are to be abolished in 1999 and hens are to be kept in free‐range conditions when producing eggs. This will push up production costs. Such eggs from ‚happy hens’ could lose out to imported eggs from hens kept in cramped hen‐coops. Many Swedes therefore wish to see origin labelling of foodstuffs introduced in the EU. The Swedish Ministry of Agriculture pressed this point in the Council.

The Commission has now been asked by the Council to investigate the possibility of introducing origin labelling. What has happened? Does the Commission consider that origin labelling of foodstuffs can be introduced in the EU.

Answer

It should be pointed out that there are already a number of Community provisions dealing with the problem of foodstuff origin. These are:

‐ Council Directive 79/112/EEC on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the labelling, presentation and advertising of foodstuffs for sale to the ultimate consumer, which requires the origin of the foodstuff to be indicated on the label in cases in which the omission of such indication would be liable to mislead the consumer about the actual origin of the foodstuff. Producers can always voluntarily indicate the origin of the foodstuff, on condition that they do not mislead the consumer by so doing.

‐ Council Regulation (EEC) No 2081/92 on the protection of geographical indications and designations of origin for agricultural products and foodstuffs, which allows producers to advertise the origin of their product in cases where the quality or characteristics of the product are influenced by its place of origin.

In the egg sector, the Community Regulations relating to marketing standards (Nos 1907/90 and 1274/91) already set out conditions under which optional indications of origin and production methods may be given. These conditions primarily concern the terms to be used and the provisions governing the control of such indications.

The Commission has no plans to introduce compulsory indication of origin for all agricultural products and foodstuffs.

***

Question No 66 by Maj Theorin (H−0485/96)

Subject: Inspection of imported meat in Sweden

At the end of May, Helsingborg environmental health office discovered 20 tonnes of salmonella‐infected chicken from France. The chicken was accompanied by a document certifying that it was free of infection. At about the same time, an inspection revealed 20 tonnes of horse meat, which had come to Sweden from Brazil via Belgium, to be infected. It, too, was accompanied by a document certifying that it was not infected. In 1995, infected imported meat was discovered in Norrköping, and it, too, was accompanied by a certificate of health from the consignor. The controls are obviously inadequate.

If the checks carried out by producers in the EU are ineffective, can Sweden reintroduce its controls on foodstuffs imported from other EU Member States?

Answer

For poultry meat destined for Finland and Sweden, Council Decision 95/411/96 established additional guarantees in relation to salmonella pursuant to the Act of Accession. These guarantees provide for microbiological spot checks in the undertaking of origin. However, for horse meat no such additional guarantees exist in relation to salmonella.

The controls at the place of destination of the products are laid down in Council Directive 89/662/EEC which regulates the veterinary‐law controls in intra‐Community trade with a view to the common internal market. Article 8 names the procedure to be applied if the Member State of destination regards the controls carried out at the place of origin as inadequate.

As is usual in such cases, the Commission will carry out an inspection to examine the situation. Until the Commission has reached its conclusions, the consignor Member State must tighten the controls on products from the undertaking in question at the request of the Member State of destination. For its part, the Member State of destination can also carry out more intensive checks on the products originating from that undertaking. In the event of a serious threat to animal or public health, the licence of the undertaking of origin may be withdrawn immediately.

Question No 67 by Joan Vallvé (H−0449/96)

Subject: Losses suffered by exporters of agricultural produce

At its May part‐session the European Parliament adopted an urgent resolution concerning attacks on lorries transporting agricultural produce through French territory.

Apart from the direct losses suffered as a result of the damage to the produce, there is another problem: the frequency of these events is undermining the confidence of buyers of the produce as regards security of supply and deadlines.

Has the Commission calculated the extent of the losses suffered by Spanish exporters? Is this taken into account when penalties are imposed on the state responsible?

Answer

The Commission shares the honourable Member's view that the violent action taken by certain groups of French farmers against fruit and vegetables originating in other Member States may not only effectively destroy the produce but may also have an indirect adverse effect on demand for the produce in question in France. This could represent an infringement of the principle of the free movement of goods and in particular Articles 30 ff of the EC Treaty.

At present, however, the Commission has no exact details on the basis of which it could assess the losses in question.

The Commission would point out that fines can only be imposed on the French state, pursuant to Article 171(2) of the EC Treaty, if it does not comply with a judgment (Article 169 EC).

As you know, the Court of Justice has not yet reached a verdict on case C−265/95.

On the other hand, anyone who has suffered losses as a result of a Member State infringing Community law may call the Member State concerned to account before a national court and obtain financial compensation. The Court of Justice of the European Community recently called attention to that right in its judgment of 5 March 1996 (in connection with cases C−46/93 ‚Brasserie du Pêcheur’ and C−48/93 ‚Factorame II’).

Question No 68 by Tommy Waidelich (H−0455/96)

Subject: CAP subsidies

A large proportion of those receiving CAP subsidies in Sweden are reported to be extremely wealthy large landowners. It is not fair that the wealthiest people benefit from EU farm subsidies whilst millions of EU citizens live in poverty.

What is the situation in the rest of the Member States as regards the granting of EU funds to wealthy farmers, and what does the Commission intend to do to reform the common agricultural policy so that subsidies are means tested?

Question No 69 by Hans Lindqvist (H−0473/96)

Subject: Farm subsidies

Sweden's annual membership fee to the EU is approximately SEK 21 billion with a certain ‚discount’ for the first few years. In 1995 the figure was approximately SEK 11 bn, paid by the Treasury. Sweden receives some of the money back in the form of aid to agriculture and for regional policy. According to the newspaper Aftonbladet , Sweden received just over SEK 4 bn in farm subsidies in 1995 but this was not returned to the Treasury. Reportedly, one fifth of this aid consists of area‐related grants to large‐scale cereal growers in Skåne. The recipients of the biggest grants were some fifty private individuals or companies that received a total of SEK 88 million between them. These grants are due to go up in 1996. Many of those subsidized are landowners, gentlemen farmers and aristocrats who often have large private fortunes. At the same time, according to other sources of information, many small farmers, particularly in northern and central Sweden, are forced to abandon farming. The number has been put at four dairy farmers a day.

Are EU farm subsidies intended to go to the gentry, private individuals and companies who are already prosperous, while small farmers are forced out of business? If not, what does the Commissioner intend to do to change this situation? Can individual Member States do anything about it themselves?

Joint answer

Since the 1992 CAP reform, a large part of the agricultural support for cereals, oilseed, protein plants and beef and veal has been provided in the form of direct payments to the farmers, while the price support in these sectors was markedly reduced. The purpose of these payments is to compensate the farmers for price cuts and they are based on the number of hectares farmed in the case of main crops and on the number of animals in the case of the beef and veal and sheepmeat sectors.

The conversion from price support to direct payments means that the agricultural support will be less of a burden on the consumer and be reflected more obviously in the EU budget. That makes it more apparent that farmers are being subsidized and that the larger undertakings receive more public subsidies than the smaller ones. Nevertheless, there is already some differentiation according to size of undertaking, even if it does not go as far as proposed by the Commission in 1991, for instance:

‐ farms with a small surface area are not obliged to set aside land;

‐ there are no particular rules relating to areas under fodder for small‐scale beef and veal producers.

Moreover, the measures to improve operating efficiency under Regulation No 2328/91 lay down size restrictions for the grant of investment aid.

The Commission set out its views on future policy in the agricultural strategy paper it adopted on 29 November 1995 and then submitted to the European Council in Madrid. The Commission takes the view that although we must encourage the agricultural sector of the European Union to become more competitive, we should also aim at establishing closer links between direct income payments on the one hand and environmental and rural development considerations on the other.

Question No 70 by Hiltrud Breyer (H−0467/96)

Subject: Unintended use of genetically engineered seed in organic farming

Authorizations for genetically engineered seed, already issued or planned and not indicating the nature of the seed, jeopardize the implementation of Regulation (EEC) No 2092/91(1) because organic farmers cannot recognize such seed and so use it unintentionally. What view does the Commissioner take of the problem and what steps will the Commission take to tackle this deplorable state of affairs?

Answer

The current provisions of Council Regulation (EEC) No 2092/91 on organic farming and the labelling of agricultural products and foodstuffs, as last amended by Regulation (EC) No 1935/95, in no way prohibit the use of genetically engineered seed provided authorization has been granted for the variety in question to enter into circulation for use in conventional farming under the Community provisions in force.

When Regulation (EC) No 1935/95 was issued the Council and the Commission in fact agreed that a more thorough investigation was needed into the potential use of genetically modified organisms (GMOs) in organic farming and that the Commission should report on this. The Commission is currently preparing that report.

At present the Council is examining the question of the official recognition of genetically modified seed varieties and aspects of the marketing of seed for use in conventional farming on the basis of a proposal from the Commission amending various Council directives on the entry into circulation of seed and plants (COM(93)598 fin). Parliament has not yet delivered its opinion on this proposal. The Commission is convinced that when the proposed measures are implemented this will considerably reduce the risk of genetically modified seed varieties being used unintentionally.

Question No 71 by Johanna Boogerd‐Quaak (H−0477/96)

Subject: Role of women in preventing rural depopulation

Reference was made at the UN Habitat II Conference to the risks of rural depopulation and migration to cities.

On 8 March Commissioner Fischler drew attention to the important role which can be played by women in preventing such trends and announced that the Commission would be submitting proposals in the framework of the equal opportunities policy.

What stage have these proposals reached? Does the Commission share my view that in the forthcoming world trade consultations both rural depopulation and the disadvantaged position of women should be discussed and is it prepared to take the initiative on this matter?

Answer

At the conference on equal opportunities and structural funds Commissioner Fischler announced measures to assist women in rural areas. ECU 20 m were to be set aside for the period 1997‐1999 to finance pilot projects under Article 8 of the EAGGF regulation for women in rural areas.

A first invitation to tender is currently under preparation and will shortly be published in the Official Journal.

These measures specifically directed at women in rural areas will be accompanied by the creation of new occupational opportunities. They are designed to combat rural depopulation, the most important aspect of our rural development policy.

As one of the events organized to discuss world trade problems, the World Trade Organization Conference of Ministers will be held in Singapore in December 1996, at which a programme of future activities may be set out. In the Commission's view, the priority questions include trade and labour standards. In this connection the Commission will urge wider application of the BIT (International Labour Office) agreements on combating discrimination at work.

During future negotiations the Commission will also concern itself with the need for an agricultural policy that can still, as far as possible, ensure the balanced distribution of activities in rural areas, in order to check the depopulation of these areas.

Question No 72 by Glenys Kinnock (H−0483/96)

Subject: Compatibility of free trade areas with the mechanisms of the common agricultural policy

Where the European Union is maintaining its efforts to negotiate free trade areas (FTAs) with third countries, how does the Commission intend to reconcile on the one hand the need to comply with Article XXIV of the GATT in those FTAs, and on the other the current requirements of the common agricultural policy?

Answer

In the context of the new WTO arrangement, free trade agreements (FTA) must essentially cover all trade and be concluded for a ten‐year period on expiry of which ‚free’ trade must be more or less guaranteed. Free trade agreements may be contested and referred to a disputes procedure within the framework of the WTO.

In the light of the new WTO arrangement and in the interests of compatibility of free trade agreements with the principles of the CAP, the Commission must assess in each case whether the economic advantages accruing to the Community as a result of reduced customs duties for a given third country market offset the costs of the reductions in duty for our own agricultural sector.

***

Question No 73 by Concepció Ferrer (H−0487/96)

Subject: Problems in the nut sector

The debate within the EP on the reform of the COM for fruit and vegetables has made clear the difficulties facing EU nut producers, who are suffering from the serious impact of preferential and customs agreements with non‐EU countries. The hazelnut sector is facing particular difficulties, since it is a monoculture in certain areas of the EU. The Commission proposal caused alarm among producers by omitting any reference to the specific support measures for the sector which had been used in the past.

The report adopted by the EP on the reform of the COM called for the special measures currently provided for in Regulations 1035/72(2) and 2159/89(3) to be continued so as to guarantee minimum incomes for producers.

In the light of growing international competition, does the Commission plan to extend the measures laid down in Regulation 2159/89 or to provide for any other specific measures or aid to protect the incomes of producers and prevent the demise of nut production?

Question No 74 by Joan Colom i Naval (H−0488/96)

Subject: Aid for nut producers

In its report on the reform of the COM for fruit and vegetables, the European Parliament called for the aid provided for under the previous regulations – which the Commission's proposal seeks to abolish – to be continued.

In the light of growing international competition, what measures does the Commission plan to take to assist nut producers and protect nut production in the Union?

Question No 75 by Carles‐Alfred Gasòliba i Böhm (H−0498/96)

Subject: Problems in the dried fruit sector

In view of the difficult situation experienced by dried fruit producers owing to the impact of the preferential and customs agreements concluded with the third countries, in particular the producers of hazelnuts (a mono‐culture in certain regions of the EU), the Commission proposal, which omits any reference to specific support measures for the sector, has provoked alarm amongst producers. The report adopted by Parliament on the reform of the COM called for the special measures currently provided for in Regulation 1035/72(4) and 2159/89(5) to be maintained.

In view of the increase in international competition, is the Commission planning to extend the measures contained in Regulation 2159/89 and establish some other kind of specific aid in accordance with the amendments adopted by Parliament in order to safeguard producers' incomes?

Joint answer

The Commission proposal on the reform of the fruit and vegetables sector is based on the existing regulation governing the nuts sector. Accordingly this proposal attributes an important function to the producer organizations in setting up a working fund and carrying out programmes to make the sector more competitive. A horizontal provision that enables the Commission to apply special measures if the other instruments prove inadequate has further extended the range of measures proposed by the Commission. Under these conditions it would be pointless to retain a special measure for nuts; in future this sector can have recourse to the horizontal provisions of the COM for fruit and vegetables.

Naturally the rights acquired by producer organizations under the existing regulation will continue to apply until they have been exhausted.

Question No 76 by Jens‐Peter Bonde (H−0491/96)

Subject: Organic farms

What percentage of all agriculture is now made up of organic farming and what percentage of all aid to agriculture from the EU budget do such farms receive?

Answer

According to the most recent and reliable information at the Commission's disposal, 0.35 % of all farms practised ecological farming in the Community of fifteen in 1993. These include farms that practise partly organic and partly conventional farming. There are signs that this percentage may have risen sharply both in 1974 and in 1975.

Both, i.e., farmers operating according to the principles of organic farming and conventional farmers, are eligible for the support measures financed under the Guarantee Section of the EAGGF under the same conditions. In view of their farming methods, there are good prospects for ‚organic’ farmers to become eligible for the following measures:

‐ financial support for environmental programmes in agriculture under Regulation (EEC) No 2078/92,

‐ financial support under Regulation (EEC) No 866/90 to improve the processing and marketing conditions of agricultural products,

‐ financial support measures for rural development in Objective 1 and Objective 5b areas under Regulation (EEC) No 2085/92.

Question No 77 by Carmen Fraga Estévez (H−0512/96)

Subject: Aid to the apiculture sector

In December 1994 the European Parliament adopted a report on the Commission communication entitled ‚Discussion paper on European apiculture’ (COM(94)256), in which it called on the Commission to submit a proposal defending the interests of the European apiculture sector. No such proposal has yet been submitted.

Will the Commission say when it intends to submit this proposal?

Answer

When in October 1994 the Council examined the Commission's discussion paper on European beekeeping, it asked the Commission to present a proposal for measures to improve the production and marketing of honey along the lines indicated in that paper without delay.

In the resolution proposed by Mrs Lulling Parliament called for a very ambitious comprehensive aid scheme for beekeeping.

The difficult situation of the Community's budget has so far not made it possible to adopt a proposal. At present a first draft is being elaborated aiming, amongst others, to assure that double financing of aid measures through different Community funds is excluded.

Question No 78 by Maren Günther (H−0376/96)

Subject: Information flow from ECHO to its NGO counterparts in all Member States concerning availability of funds in crisis situations

Whenever ECHO decides in crisis situations to make available a given sum of money to finance NGO humanitarian actions, the information to that effect frequently fails to reach NGOs in the Member States. The result is that NGOs in peripheral EU Member States in particular are significantly under‐represented in EU humanitarian aid contracts.

Does ECHO intend to set up a rapid information system (e.g. e‐mail) with its NGO counterparts in the Member States, and if so, how soon could that be expected? Does ECHO intend to hold meetings at regular intervals with its NGO counterparts to inform them about forthcoming actions and financial decisions to be taken annually?

Answer

In the event of a humanitarian crisis and when it comes to deciding on humanitarian actions, the Commission endeavours to seek the most competent and efficient partners so as to ensure that the vital needs of the victims are met and that they receive help, often in situations of conflict.

The Commission tries to avail itself of the widest possible range of competent partner organizations across the Community. The Commission also encourages technology transfers from the traditional NGOs with years of experience to more recently created NGOs.

In addition, the Commission is trying to enhance the transparency of its actions, and during the 1995 financial year half of the administered funds were actually allocated to operations carried out by NGOs; in the course of 1995 an increased effort was devoted to close coordination with partners and to the organization of briefing and working sessions with NGOs from particular Member States with a view to making it easier to convey information on the procedures and priorities of humanitarian assistance.

This effort will be continued, as will the special contacts with VOICE, the channel of representation and coordination for a large number of NGOs operating in the field of humanitarian aid.

In due course, technological development and the capacities of partner organizations in terms of information technology should result in more extensive use of e‐mail.

At the present time, however, there are no plans to introduce a rapid information system to inform all NGOs in Member States systematically of forthcoming actions, even though the Commission does generally inform partner organizations which are involved in dealing with a given crisis before proposing guidelines for the next operational phase, and their suggestions are listened to carefully. Nor is it unusual for operations to be assigned to a new operator.

None the less, it must be remembered that the paramount aim of humanitarian aid has to be effective help for the victims of conflicts and natural disasters and that it is only right to expect partners to possess competence and expertise in their field.

Question No 79 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0406/96)

Subject: Waste management in Attica

An alarming environmental and public health situation is developing in Athens and other areas of Attica where refuse is not being collected owing to protests by local council workers. Local residents and the local authorities are also complaining that the lack of an effective waste management policy has an adverse impact on the economy, exacerbates pollution and gives rise to dozens of illegal rubbish dumps in the Attica Basin and surrounding area.

In view of the public health hazards involved, especially during the hot summer months with the risk of fire from the illegal rubbish dumps, what measures will the Commission take to remedy this situation immediately in compliance with the relevant Community legislation and on the basis of the available studies and the decision taken by the general assembly of local authorities in Attica?

Answer

According to Article 4 of Directive 75/442/EEC, as amended by Directive 91/156/EEC, ’The Member States shall take the necessary measures to ensure that waste is recovered or dispose of without endangering human health and without using processes or methods which could harm the environment, and in particular:

‐ without risk to water, air, soil and plants and animals;

‐ without causing a nuisance through noise or odours;

‐ without adversely affecting the countryside or places of special interest.

Member States shall also take the necessary measures to prohibit the abandonment, dumping or uncontrolled disposal of waste’.

Article 7 of the same Directive requires Member States to draw up waste‐management plans.

The Court of Justice has already ruled that Greece violated Community law by failing to adopt plans for the management of hazardous waste in a prefecture of Crete. Greece has yet to act on this judgment of 1992 (C45/91). And indeed, the Commission has not received these plans to date.

Proceedings under Article 171 for non‐compliance with the judgment of the Court are now pending.

Question No 80 by Jesús Cabezón Alonso (H−0409/96)

Subject: Use of drift gill nets

If the total ban on the use of drift gill nets is not unanimously adopted by the Council of Ministers, would the Commission be prepared to submit to the Council another, amended, proposal which distinguishes between fishing in the Baltic and fishing in the Atlantic and the Mediterranean, provided that the intention to ban such nets in the open sea is maintained?

Answer

The Commission introduced its proposal to phase out the use of driftnets in April 1994. During the last two years the Council has had more than enough time to reach a majority on this or a modified version, but has so far failed to do so.

The Commission has expressed on a number of occasions its preparedness to associate itself with any proposal in the Council that, while respecting the basic principle of the Commission's original proposal, would help reaching a majority. However, this possible majority on a modified proposal has not emerged either.

On this basis, the Commission considers that a new proposal would face the same problem, the lack of a majority position, and such a new proposal would only provide Member States with an excuse to delay the adoption of the ban on driftnets even further.

***

Question No 81 by James Elles (H−0411/96)

Subject: Human rights standards in Turkey

What assurance has the EU been given by the Turkish Government about upholding international human rights standards, especially in its relations with the Kurds?

Answer

The political crisis which has been raging in Turkey since the elections of 24 December 1995 has made it difficult for the Turkish Parliament to adopt further reforms, particularly with regard to the continuation of the democratization process and the Kurdish question. The resignation of the Prime Minister, Mr Yilmaz, on 6 June 1996 has sparked off a fresh political crisis, the outcome of which is uncertain.

As regards the process of democratization, the programme drawn up by Mr Yilmaz's coalition government, which had been in power since March 1996, contained precise objectives, foremost among which was eliminate from Turkish legislation any obstacles to freedom of expression.

As regards the Kurdish question, Mr Yilmaz had made important statements since taking office and had emphasized in particular that the issue could only be solved by peaceful, and not by military, means. Among the plans he announced were the progressive lifting of the state of emergency in south‐east Turkey and the possible privately funded provision of education in the Kurdish language.

However, because of the difficulties encountered by the governing coalition and the intractability of the political crisis in Turkey, these plans have not taken shape.

During Commissioner van den Broek's visit to Turkey in April 1996 and in the course of the talks which the President of the Commission, Mr Santer, had with Mr Yilmaz on 4 June in Brussels, the Commission took the opportunity to remind its Turkish partners of the importance it attaches to the continuation of the human rights reforms which Turkey initiated last year and the implementation of other such measures which have been announced. Mr van den Broek also raised the matter recently with the Turkish Foreign Minister, Mr Gönensay.

***

Question No 82 by Christine Oddy (H−0413/96)

Subject: Homeworking and proposed new ILO convention on homeworking

Is the Commission aware that a new ILO convention on homeworking will be voted on at the forthcoming ILO conference in June?

What steps will the Commission take to implement the proposed new ILO convention?

Answer

The question of ‚Homework’ is on the agenda of the 83rd session of the International Labour Conference that will take place in Geneva from 4 to 20 June 1996. This will be the second discussion on this subject.

The European Commission has the status of an observer at the Conference and will follow the discussions with great interest. The Commission would welcome the adoption of an international labour convention and a recommendation on Homework. It will take due account of the outcome of the Conference in its future action in this field.

Question No 83 by Raymonde Dury (H−0414/96)

Subject: Subsidies for regional languages

The Commission (DG XXII) has refused to subsidize the publication of a collection of poems by the Aromanian poet Matilda Caragiu. The text in the original language, Aromanian, a Romanian language spoken in the Balkans, was to have been accompanied by translations into Spanish, French and Italian. It seems that some projects for other regional languages have been given extensive support. Can the Commission indicate the criteria used in agreeing or refusing to provide subsidies for any given regional language?

Answer

In line with resolutions adopted by the European Parliament, the Commission is implementing a measure to promote regional and minority languages and cultures of the Community.

The only languages eligible to benefit from this measure are native languages traditionally spoken by part of the population of a Member State or of the EEA. This definition covers neither the languages of immigrants nor artificially created languages.

The selection of projects is based on precise criteria published by the Commission in the Official Journal of 2 December 1995, under reference number 95/C 332/13. According to these criteria, selection of a project is subject to its having a European dimension, creating a cascade effect and being of urgent importance; other criteria relate to the quality and presentation of the request and the cost‐benefit ratio. As a general rule, each project is assessed on the basis of all the criteria.

Question No 84 by Arthur Newens (H−0416/96)

Subject: Transport and regional policy implications of the environment of railway termini

Can the Commissioner for Transport and the Commissioner for Regional Affairs provide information on any joint work which is being undertaken by their directorates on the implications for both transport and regional policy of the issues of social exclusion, poor quality physical environment and visitor management which are a common feature of railway termini in all European Union cities?

Answer

The Commission is concerned about the state of many railway stations in the Community, because of the social situation it reflects and because it reduces the attractiveness of rail transport to the citizens.

This goes against the Commission's aim of promoting an environmentally friendly form of transport, its policy of encouraging collective transport that offers a real alternative to the private car, the Citizens' Network and the action programme for accessible public transport.

However, the management of stations is the competence of the railways and of the ministries that supervise them, not of the Community. Consequently, it is up to them to improve the state of railway stations.

The Commission obviously hopes that measures will be taken, where necessary, to improve the aspect of railway stations.

Question No 85 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0417/96)

Subject: Underground line and the antiquities of the Cemetery of the Kerameikos

In her reply to my question P−3654/95 concerning the route taken by the Athens Underground through the antiquities of Kerameikos, the competent Commissioner, Mrs Wulf‐Mathies, conveyed an assurance that all necessary test drillings and surveys would be carried out to avoid the risk of damaging the antiquities. Bearing in mind the damage caused to the Wall of Valerian – another important archaeological monument – despite timely written warnings from the Department of Antiquities (C), does the Commission know the results of the test drillings? If the monuments are not safe, is consideration being given to the possibility of re‐routing the Underground so as to avoid this archaeologically sensitive area? And if the archaeological monuments are damaged through inadequate compliance with safety rules, could the Greek State claim compensation under the project contract to repair the damage?

Answer

The Commission has asked ‚Attiko Metro’, the organization responsible for the construction of the project, to give precise information on the measures taken to protect the Kerameikos ancient cemetery. ‚Attiko Metro’ has confirmed these three points:

‐ first, the design now provides for the tunnel to pass at a depth of 10 metres under the cemetery, instead of 5 metres as provided for in the preliminary design. This increase in depth had been recommended by the archaeology service, and it reduces to a minimum possible risks for antiquities;

‐ second, a thorough study of soil conditions under the cemetery is in hand and should be completed very shortly. On the basis of this study a group of experts, including well‐known engineers and specialists to be invited by the Ministry of Public Works, will suggest supplementary measures to be taken, so as to avoid the slightest risk for antiquities;

‐ third, the possible measures that could be taken if necessary include soil improvement, further increase of the depth of the tunnel or its horizontal displacement.

The Commission will continue to monitor the situation and keep the honourable Member informed.

Question No 86 by Rui Vieira (H−0419/96)

Subject: Poseima

Poseima is a wide‐ranging programme for the autonomous regions of Madeira and the Azores. It includes various measures which have quite rightly been of benefit to agriculture, fisheries, handicrafts, energy, consumers, the environment, taxation, etc.

I imagine that the Commission will seek to ensure that this programme is continued, if only because the text establishing it makes no general reference to expiry dates. However, in some cases, the periods for the granting of aid are stipulated, e.g. in the case of the extra cost of the maritime transport of petroleum products.

Poseima should also cover other products with a major impact on the economy of these outermost regions, e.g. agricultural fertilizers, building materials (cement, timber, roofing materials, glass, etc.) and components of livestock feedingstuffs, which have so far not been covered.

Does the Commission plan to improve the effectiveness of Poseima by continuing to support the extra cost of the transport of petroleum products, extending the expiry dates for other products and including additional products essential to the economy such as those mentioned above?

Answer

As the honourable Member rightly emphasizes, the POSEIMA programme, the purpose of which is to adjust the application of common policies to make them better adapted to the particular circumstances of the Azores and Madeira, is not of limited duration. It is intended as an evolving framework, with the flexibility to adjust, as the need arises, the specific measures it supports. That is why the Commission, in partnership with the Portuguese authorities and at their request, is currently preparing a revision of the agricultural element of POSEIMA. The agricultural products cited by the honourable Member have not hitherto been the subject of a special request by the Portuguese authorities within the scope of this revision.

The semimanufactures and industrial raw materials referred to by the honourable Member are not covered by a supply system equivalent to that established by POSEIMA for agricultural produce. Nevertheless, they may benefit from Council Regulation (EC) No 122/96, which establishes preferential tariff treatment for the importation of certain goods to the autonomous regions of the Azores and Madeira by reason of their intended purpose.

The assistance designed to compensate for the extra cost of transporting petroleum products by sea to which the honourable Member referred was established by the POSEIMA programme for the years 1991 to 1993 inclusive. Thereafter, the renewal of this measure was thwarted by problems relating to eligibility for structural funding. Nevertheless, the Commission, which is aware of the considerable dependence of Madeira and the Azores on external energy sources, will continue its efforts to find suitable means of maintaining fair energy prices in these two Community regions.

Question No 87 by Isidoro Sánchez García (H−0420/96)

Subject: EU‐Cuban relations

What, in the Commission's view, is the current state of relations between the European Union and Cuba?

Answer

The Commission wishes to remind the honourable Member that the European Council, at its meeting in Madrid on 14 and 15 December of last year, adopted the following conclusions concerning relations with Cuba:

’It considers it that dialogue and cooperation should be continued with Cuba in order to lend active support to the process of reform under way, to foster respect for human rights and fundamental freedoms and to broaden the scope of private initiative and the development of civil society. To that end, it asks the Commission to present, in the first half of 1996, draft negotiating directives for a trade and economic cooperation agreement, which will be examined by the Council in the light of developments in the political and economic situation in Cuba’.

With a view to exploring the possibilities of presenting draft negotiating guidelines for a new agreement on economic and commercial cooperation with Cuba, the European Commission has maintained contacts with the Cuban authorities in order to discuss the political and economic matters referred to in the aforementioned conclusions.

Following these contacts, and in view of the responses provided by its Cuban partners, it appears that the Commission will not be in a position to present the Council with draft negotiating guidelines for an agreement with Cuba within the period envisaged by the European Council.

So that it can obtain all useful information and assess the prospects of presenting a set of draft negotiating guidelines in keeping with the wishes of the European Council, the Commission takes the view that the present dialogue and contacts should be pursued and intensified.

The European Commission has already communicated this position to the Council of Ministers.

Question No 88 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0434/96)

Subject: Violation of Union legislation governing maritime transport within Member States

During the first two weeks in May 1996, the ‚Marco Polo’, a cruise ship flying the Bahamian flag, flagrantly violated Greek legislation governing maritime transport within Member States (cabotage) by organizing cruises departing from Piraeus with the – unacceptable – complicity of the Greek Government, which also adopted a stance that is incompatible with the provisions set out in Council Regulation (EEC) No 3577/92 of 7 December 1992(6) of which in particular Article 6(3) taken in conjunction with the second subparagraph of Article 4(1) were infringed.

Since, if the Greek Government adopted the same attitude towards ships flying flags of convenience or, more generally, the flag of a third country on a subsequent occasion, it would cause a serious disturbance on the internal transport market – in Greece, where the right to work of Greek seamen, services to the islands and the country's national defence would be jeopardized, as well as in other Member States of the Union (France, Spain and Portugal) – within the meaning of Article 5(1) of the Regulation referred to above, can the Commission say whether it takes the view that the ‚Marco Polo’ did violate Community legislation and whether it intends to adopt safeguard measures on its own initiative in accordance with Article 5(2) of the Regulation referred to above? If so, what measures will it take?

Answer

The information provided by the honourable Member is insufficient to allow the Commission to give a definitive reply to the question. Further information that would be required relates to the final destination of the ‚Marco Polo’ and to any intermediate ports at which it may have called. This information is needed for the following reason:

Article 6.2 of Regulation 3577/92 applying the freedom to provide services to maritime transport within Member States (maritime cabotage) allows the five southern Member States (Greece, France, Spain, Portugal, Italy) to reserve cabotage services in island traffic to shipowners using national flag vessels until 1 January 1999. Notwithstanding this, it is up to each Member State to decide whether it wants to liberalise such services in advance of the calendar foreseen in the regulation.

On the other hand, Regulation 4055/86 applying the same principle of freedom to provide services to maritime transport between Member States and between Member States and third countries allows shipping companies of Member States to provide such services using ships flying a third country flag as from 1 January 1993. If the port of final destination were outside Greece and no passengers had embarked and subsequently disembarked in a Greek port, this regulation would apply.
