Question No 89 by Per Gahrton (H−0451/96)

Subject: Sabotaging of EU environmental policy by big business

It was reported in the Danish daily Børsen of 29 May that large oil and chemicals conglomerates had secretly joined forces in an attempt to sabotage EU environmental policies (including ecotaxes), for instance by bribing researchers working for the Commission, and that bribes had allegedly been offered to Stef Proost, a Belgian economics professor at Louvain University.

Is the Commission aware of systematic attempts by big business to sabotage its environmental policies? What steps will it take to thwart attempts by wealthy industrialists hostile to environmental protection to exert improper influence over its experts?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the article to which the honourable Member refers. However, the statements contained in the Børsen newspaper are only partly accurate. The article contains an incomplete account of facts which were reported to the Commission in good time, namely the desire of a number of enterprises to finance a research programme, to be undertaken by the Catholic University of Louvain, into the economic impact of a tax on carbon dioxide.

The Catholic University of Louvain is currently participating, along with other research teams, in the development of a European model designed to provide insight into the interaction of the economy, energy and the environment (GEM−3E) and financed under the JOULE III research programme. Subject to a favourable response from the Commission to the aforementioned overtures, one of several conditions on which its participation in the GEM−3E project depends, the University of Louvain suggested that the model developed so far be used to carry out simulations on the research subject proposed by the enterprises.

While it appreciates offers from industry to cooperate with research institutes, the Commission rejected this request, the purpose of which was not to modify the model but to use it at a time when its technical development had not been completed. This decision is explained on the one hand by the need to have the model reviewed by independent experts and on the other hand by the fact that research requests similar to those made by the said enterprises are already one of the objectives of the JOULE III research programme, the interim findings of which are due to be presented to interested parties around the month of October 1996.

Moreover, the Commission believes it possesses adequate means to protect itself against actions that might undermine its environmental policy and that these means have stood the test, as the aforementioned case illustrates in relation to R&D. One such instrument is the obligation to seek authorization before publishing or exploiting the results of Community research, and failure to honour it can result in sanctions. In addition, the Commission operates a system of quality certification of models and their results, carried out by top experts who are not involved in the programmes. The combined effect of these two instruments is to bolster confidence in the technical studies we commission.

Finally, the Commission naturally exercises political control over environmental actions, a control which goes beyond the research underlying its environmental policy.

The Commission attaches high priority to the improvement of nuclear safety. To take account of these concerns about nuclear safety, a specific joint declaration was inserted during the negotiations on the establishment of an effective system for exchanging information in the event of a radiation emergency.

Subject: Mechanically Recovered Meat – timetable for discussions

Further to the Commission reply QE 0877/96 of 20 May 1996 regarding Mechanically Recovered Meat (MRM), I understand that at least one Member State government believes the matter to be in the hands of the Commission rather than in a period of discussion. Given that the BSE crisis has highlighted the need for action on MRM and for clear public information on meat products, what exactly is the timetable for Commission and Member State progress on labelling and by which date can Members expect to be informed?

Answer

As was pointed out by the Commission in its answer to the honourable Member's written question E−0877/96, the representatives of the Commission and the experts from the Member States in the working party on labelling reached agreement on the fact that the presence of mechanically recovered meat in a food product should be specifically indicated in the list of ingredients.

Question No 92 by Lone Dybkjær (H−0462/96/rev. 1)

Subject: Directive on personal data processing

Can the Commission confirm that it is consistent with the directive on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and of the free movement of such data (Directive 95/46/EC of 24 October 1995)(7) for the authority responsible for the register in the individual countries to require, when commissioning a third party to operate such an EDP register – not least centralized registers of personal data and registers maintained by the defence forces and police – that the register should actually be run and operated in that authority's country, regardless of the authority's obligation to exchange certain data with the authorities of other EU Member States?

Answer

Article 1 of Directive 95/46/EC of 24 October 1995 prohibits restrictions to the free flow of personal data between Member States for reasons to do with data protection.

However, the directive does not apply to data processing concerning public security, defence, state security and areas outside the scope of Community law.

The substantive harmonization of data protection provisions which will result from this directive, once it is implemented in Member States, includes a requirement for appropriate security measures of both a technical and organizational nature. On the basis of the information given, the Commission is not in a position to judge whether such security measures might occasionally include requirements as to the physical location of a database if this were an appropriate response to a particular security risk.

Question No 93 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0463/96)

Subject: Commission security office

In a television documentary made by Sweden's TV−4, it is claimed that the Commission has its own security office with a staff of about 80, headed by Pieter de Haan, which employs methods such as shadowing, telephone tapping and the opening of mail with the aim of keeping Commission staff under surveillance. During the programme, a Commission trade union representative confirms that the security office exists.

Will the Commission confirm or deny that it has its own security office? If it confirms the claim, is it prepared to explain to Parliament the extent of the office's activities and the size of its budget and indicate the budget item used for financing the security service?

Answer

The Commission, like the international organizations and the other institutions, has a security office, which is responsible for the protection of persons and property in Brussels, Luxembourg and all other places of employment. This service features in the organization chart of the Commission and acts on the authority of the President. It has an establishment of 83 posts.

Its activities are defined by a Commission decision of 8 September 1994.

The Security Office administers part of the budgetary allocation under item A−205 – Security and surveillance of buildings.

Question No 95 by Heidi Hautala (H−0466/96)

Subject: Forest certification

Various efforts to create a certification system for forests and forestry products are being taken by industry, non‐governmental organizations and governments. Certification is a flexible market‐based instrument which is needed to transfer environmental information from producers to consumers. An essential part of the work is to create criteria for sustainable management of forests. However, at present there are disagreements between the various actors and interest groups involved in forestry about how certification should be organized and authorized.

How does the Commission view the certification of forests and forestry products, and what role does it see for itself in this matter?

Answer

The Commission has followed the emergence of various efforts to create certification systems for forests and forest products. Certification is seen as one tool among others which may help to reinforce sustainable forest management and even encourage its wider application. The Commission is currently discussing Community policy options with regard to forest and forest product certification as well as other measures with the experts of the Member States and other interested socio‐economic actors. Options under examination are:

(a) to rely on market forces to establish certification, but to facilitate their action;
(b) to contribute actively to the development and definition of standards for sustainable forest management, which would be used as the basis for certification;
(c) to set up a Community level certification scheme, tackling the proliferation of schemes;
(d) to consider other instruments, such as trade measures or forest registration.

A combination of options would also be possible. Whilst it is not yet clear which option might emerge as that to be pursued, the Commission recognizes that any certification scheme operating within the Community should comply with the principles of being:

‐ credible

‐ voluntary

‐ effective and cost effective

‐ transparent

‐ non‐discriminatory, as well as

‐ avoiding unnecessary trade restrictions.

Furthermore, it is essential that internationally agreed reference criteria for sustainable forest management be developed which can be used as a common reference for the development of national, regional, or local management unit level criteria. The Commission believes that in Europe the most appropriate framework for the development of such basic reference criteria is the pan‐European follow‐up process to the Helsinki Ministerial Conference on the Protection of Forests in Europe.

As far as the Community's actions to promote tropical forests are concerned, the Council Regulation (EC) No 3062/95 on operations to promote tropical forests gives particular consideration to the definition and development of certification systems, combined with independent assessment systems, for timber produced from tropical forests according to sustainable forest management principles as part of internationally harmonized certification systems envisaged for all kinds of timber and timber products. Protocol 10 to the Lomé Convention on Sustainable Management of Forest Resources also provides for Community support in this context. The Commission is supporting several research and pilot projects in the field of the development of certification systems.

At the international level discussion fora such as the Intergovernmental Panel on Forests (IPF) under the UN Commission for Sustainable Development (UN‐CSD) is currently dealing with the certification issue. Discussions on trade and environment under the World Trade Organization (WTO) are also of great relevance. The Commission is actively following and contributing to these discussions.

***

Question No 96 by Niels Sindal (H−0468/96)

Subject: Patent protection

Patent holders frequently find that their patents are copied by both private‐ and public‐sector undertakings and consequently cut down on further development work, as the return from their investment is diminished.

Will the Commission assist such patent holders, for instance by making access to legal aid simpler and cheaper or by imposing stiffer penalties?

Answer

The Commission is well aware of the fact that one of the key elements of industrial property law is the effectiveness of the legal procedures required for its proper implementation and observance.

The agreement on the aspects of intellectual property law which relate to commerce, signed within the GATT framework and in force since 1 January 1996 in the Member States of the Community, contains several provisions that answer the honourable Member's concerns.

The first requirement is to provide for fair and equitable civil procedures so as to enable patentees to assert their rights to intellectual property.

In this context there is currently an instrument, not yet widely used, which helps to reduce litigation costs and is especially useful to SMEs, namely legal expenses insurance for intellectual property.

With regard to sanctions for violation of intellectual property rights, the agreement lays down that offenders will have to pay adequate damages and may also be ordered to pay expenses incurred by the patentee, particularly lawyer's fees, which can also reduce the costs borne by the patentee.

Finally, as regards sentencing, the agreement lays down that sanctions are to include imprisonment and/or fines high enough to act as a deterrent, as well as, where appropriate, seizure, confiscation and destruction of pirated goods.

It must, however, be noted that, under Community law as it stands, the judicial enforcement of intellectual property rights falls within the jurisdiction of the Member States.

Question No 97 by Juan Izquierdo Collado (H−0475/96)

Subject: Conditions imposed on the Structural Funds

Does the Commission maintain that the Structural Funds should be required to meet the convergence criteria or any other condition apart from the one that they should be correctly used in accordance with the rules?

Answer

At the informal meeting of Ministers for Economy and Finance held in Verona in April 1996, the Commission was asked to examine ways of strengthening efforts to promote economic convergence in the Community including the possibility of introducing macroeconomic conditionality into the Structural Funds. The Commission will present a report before the summer break.

The principles governing the Structural Funds are set in the current regulations which expire on 31 December 1999. The Commission does not intend to propose changes for the present programming period 1994‐1999.

For the future, the extension of the principle of conditionality – which is applicable at present only of the Cohesion Fund – to the Structural Funds must be considered in the light of its economic consequences and its practical implications.

It must also take account of the specific objectives of Community policies under the Structural Funds. Unlike the Cohesion Fund, which was conceived as an instrument to support national budgets and to facilitate the achievement of the nominal convergence criteria for participation in economic and monetary union, the Structural Funds address the problems of the less favoured regions and social groups in order to reduce socioeconomic disparities and to promote real convergence.

Subject: Blocking of EU business by the UK

During the past month the UK Government has blocked a series of decisions (on European Year against Racism, equal participation of men and women in decision‐taking, the simplification of Community legislation, the protection of the economic interests of the Community, etc.) by evoking national interests to an unwarranted degree.

Question No 100 by Jan Andersson (H−0478/96)

Subject: The common agricultural policy

We are constantly being told that competitiveness must increase if unemployment is to be reduced. In order to achieve that goal, efforts must be concentrated in sectors which will play a significant role in the future, such as telecommunications, the environment and new transport technologies.

In reality, however, about half of the EU's budget goes on agriculture.

Ought it not be possible to gradually reduce support for agriculture and instead increase the funding of various future‐oriented sectors, so as to bring down unemployment?

Answer

1. The development of the Community budget up to and including 1999 is governed by the financial perspective that is annexed to the interinstitutional agreement concluded on 29 October 1993 by the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission. This is the framework within which any potential reallocation of expenditure has to be examined.

In the financial perspective, the ceiling for agricultural expenditure is defined by the agricultural guideline, the annual increase in which is limited to 74 % of the increase in Community GNP. This provision therefore results automatically in a reduction in the percentage of the budget devoted to agricultural expenditure. So in fact this expenditure develops less quickly than economic growth, whereas the ceiling for resources put at the disposal of the Community will develop more quickly, increasing from 1.2 % to 1.27 % of GNP.

Moreover, since the agricultural guideline constitutes an expenditure ceiling, economies have been made in the implementation of the CAP in the course of the last few years. These amounted to over two billion ECUs in the 1995 financial year, for example.

2. Designed as a mechanism of budgetary discipline, the financial perspective prevents the use of savings made under one heading to meet needs that arise under another heading, unless the two branches of the budgetary authority agree otherwise when revising the financial framework.

In this respect, it must be remembered that on 20 March of this year the Commission presented the budgetary authority with a proposed revision of the financial perspective.(8) The main purpose of this proposal, indeed, is to help strengthen competitiveness and growth by increasing the financial resources available for internal policies on trans‐European networks, research and SMEs.

In view of the constraints on public finances in the Member States, the Commission has not proposed an increase in the overall expenditure ceiling, but instead suggested redeployment of some of the anticipated savings from the agricultural sector in 1998 and 1999. In concrete terms, this revision would be funded by the progressive transfer of expenditure, currently posted under headings 2 and 3, under the agricultural guideline. The introduction of new budget lines alongside those allocated to the EAGGF Guarantee Section under the agricultural guideline is certainly one way of exploiting the surplus funds that may accrue in the agricultural budget.

This proposal is currently being examined by the budgetary authority.

Question No 101 by Robert Evans (H−0481/96)

Subject: EP funds to IPPF

Exactly how much does the EU give to the International Planned Parenthood Federation (IPPF) and what is this money used for?

More specifically does this money go directly to projects in China or form part of the IPPF general budget?

Answer

In 1993, the Commission agreed with the International Planned Parenthood Federation ‚Support to IPPF to provide family planning to reduce fertility in the Southern and Eastern Mediterranean countries’. This was a grant under the budget heading B7‐4080 ‚Operations promoting multilateral and regional or sub‐regional cooperation in the Mediterranean’.

Under the project family planning associations from Morocco, Tunisia, Algeria, Egypt, Gaza and West‐Bank, Jordan Lebanon and Turkey implement integrated activities targeting the reduction of fertility, improvement of family planning, reproductive, sexual and maternal child health services, and the empowerment of women.

There has been no Commission support to IPPF projects in China.

Question No 102 by John Iversen (H−0492/96)

Subject: Duty on sweetener, xylitol

The Commission has announced that Finland's exemption from duty of sweets containing xylitol constitutes a distortion of competition. The matter is now to be investigated by a scientific committee, which seems totally out of proportion considering that the original initiative came from a group of school children exerting influence over their own lives and health.

Will the Commission therefore provide me with an estimate of the expected cost of combating the Finnish children's initiative to have healthy sweets?

Answer

First of all, the Commission wishes to reiterate that it applauds the desire of the children at Vatiala school to influence the legislative process and promote healthier lifestyles. The Commissioner responsible for financial matters had the opportunity to meet them on 22 April, and he was delighted to do so. This was the occasion for a warm exchange of views, which was greatly appreciated by both sides.

In the meantime, the Commission has been able to examine in depth the documentation submitted by the Finnish authorities and has concluded that it is inappropriate to submit the question of xylitol to the Scientific Committee on Human Nutrition.

In fact, the latter body has already endorsed the use of xylitol as a sweetener in foodstuffs on the same basis as similar sweeteners such as sorbitol, mannitol, isomalt, maltitol and lactitol.

On the other hand, the use of one or other of these substances as such is not what prevents the formation of dental caries; only a reduction in sugar (sucrose) intake can do that.

Subject: Marketing of Scotch whisky in Chile

Will the Commission undertake to resolve the difficulties surrounding the marketing of Scotch whisky in Chile during the current trade agreement being negotiated?

Question No 104 by Karin Riis‐Jørgensen (H−0502/96)

Subject: Cocoa‐producing developing countries

In connection with the current simplification of Directive 78/241/EEC(9) on cocoa and chocolate products, a proposal has been put forward to allow 5 % of vegetable fat to be added to chocolate products which may have adverse repercussions for cocoa‐producing developing countries.

In the light of the Development Ministers' meeting of 28 May 1996 which took a decision to investigate the impact on the cocoa‐producing developing countries, will the Commission say what benefits can be expected for the developing countries producing vegetable oil, e.g. in the form of more market outlets, with particular reference to the wild‐growing shia nut?

Answer

The Commission is fully aware of the consequences that its proposed revision of the directive on cocoa and chocolate products may have on developing countries, be they cocoa producers or producers of other vegetable fats used in chocolate production, such as shia butter. For some African countries such as Burkina Faso and Mali, trade in these seed products is a key economic activity.

In preparing its proposal, the Commission carried out an analysis of the potential economic repercussions for these countries. However, it came to the conclusion that, under the present circumstances, it was impossible to obtain reliable estimates.

In this respect, the Commission wishes to emphasize that the terms of its proposal give Member States the option of authorizing or prohibiting the use of vegetable fats other than cocoa butter. Under these conditions, it is not possible for the Commission to assess the precise economic impact of its proposal, because that depends on the reactions of Member States, chocolate manufacturers and consumers.

According to the information currently available, the application of the principle of mutual recognition should not lead to any change in the immediate future in the consumption of chocolate containing only cocoa butter and of chocolate containing other vegetable fats.

***

Question No 105 by Philippe De Coene (H−0503/96)

Subject: European financing of golf courses

The reply to my question H−0382/96(10) was very inadequate. I should therefore like to know under what budget line six golf courses have received European aid for ‚ecologically sound’ extensions. If the Oudenaarde golf club, to which my original question referred, is not receiving aid from the ERDF, what is the source of this EU subsidy? Which service is responsible for this? Was this subsidy arrangement announced in the Official Journal, and in what number? What are the six golf courses that have been chosen and what sum has each of them received? Who chose them and on what basis? Is the Commission prepared to review its decision concerning the golf courses?

Answer

No funds from Budget line B4/306 have been paid to any golf club. In the past some financial support was given to the European Golf Association Ecology Unit to allow for the employment of ecology consultants to run a pilot exercise on the ecological aspects of golf course management. For this work, eight golf courses were examined, but far from receiving EC funds, the clubs in question contributed towards meeting the cost of the overall exercise.

***

Question No 106 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H−0507/96)

Subject: Doubts expressed by Turkey about the Greek character of Gavdos

At a meeting of NATO's military committee in Naples, Turkey went so far as to cast doubt officially on the Greek character of Gavdos, a small island with 300 inhabitants which lies off the south‐western coast of Crete and is the most southerly point of the European Union. Given that Turkish aggressiveness towards Greece, a Member State of the Union, is constantly escalating and assuming ever more improbable dimensions, will the Commission say how it views this particular matter, and what measures it intends to take to prevail upon Turkey finally to cease constantly and systematically disrupting the political climate in this sensitive part of the Mediterranean?

Answer

The Commission has been informed of this matter, which has caused some concern, and it has asked the Turkish authorities for clarification.

The explanation received from the latter is that this matter stems from a misunderstanding and that Turkey does not contest Greek sovereignty over the island of Gavdos.

***

Question No 107 by Luciano Vecchi (H−0284/96)

Subject: Obstacles to importing ceramic tiles into Hungary

With effect from 1 April 1996 Hungary intends to introduce new and unacceptable arrangements involving controls on imports of ceramic tiles, based on a complex system of licences, which are contrary to all international trade agreements.

What steps does the Commission intend to take to avoid a situation which would seriously jeopardize the ceramics production sector of the European Union Member States?

Answer

The Commission is fully aware that any measure affecting trade in ceramic tiles between the Community and some of its partners in central and eastern Europe, such as Hungary, is a sensitive matter.

For that reason, the Commission is closely monitoring this situation with a view to ensuring that Hungary is effectively abiding by the provisions of the Europe Agreement. If it transpired that there was a breach of the Agreement, the Commission would certainly refer the matter to the competent bodies.

In the meantime, the Commission has already been in touch with the Hungarian authorities, which have undertaken to notify it formally of all relevant details.

***

Question No 108 by Anne McIntosh (H−0333/96)

Subject: Equalization of pensions and the ECJ

Does the Commission recognize that the current uncertainty with regard to the European Court of Justice's ruling on the equalization of pensions needs to be clarified?

Will the Commission seek to clarify this by legislation, in order to end the uncertainty currently facing the pension industry and European pension holders?

Answer

The Commission has already presented a proposal for a Council Directive (COM(95) 186 final of 16.05.95) modifying Directive 86/378/EEC relating to the equality of treatment between men and women in occupational social security schemes, with a view to clarifying the law applicable in this area.

The aim of this proposal is to ensure consistency with Article 119 of the EC Treaty as it has been interpreted by the Court of Justice in the Barber judgement of 17 May 1990 and the subsequent judgements. As Article 119 of the Treaty is a provision of primary law of direct effect, the proposed amendments only serve to transpose the case law of the Court of Justice, and this proposal for a Directive is of a purely declaratory nature.

The Economic and Social Committee has already given its opinion on the Commission's proposal and the European Parliament is presently examining it. The Commission understands that the Council will shortly begin its work.
